<p><b>Qin Shi Huang</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%A7%A6" title="wikt:秦">秦</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A7%8B%E7%9A%87" title="wikt:始皇">始皇</a></span>, <span style="font-size:85%;"><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qin_shi_huang_pronunciation_2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/Qin_shi_huang_pronunciation_2.ogg" title="Qin shi huang pronunciation 2.ogg">pronunciation</a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Qin_shi_huang_pronunciation_2.ogg" title="File:Qin shi huang pronunciation 2.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span></span>; 259–210 BC) was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> and the first <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperor</a> of a unified China.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMüller2021"Introduction"_8-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMüller2021"Introduction"-8'>[6]</a></sup> Rather than maintain the title of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_king" title="Chinese king">king</a>" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">王</span></span> <i>wáng</i>) borne by the previous <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a> rulers, he ruled as the First Emperor (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">始皇帝</span></span>) of the Qin dynasty from 221 to 210 BC. His self-invented title "emperor" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">皇帝</span></span> <i><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zh-hu%C3%A1ngd%C3%AC.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Zh-hu%C3%A1ngd%C3%AC.ogg" title="Zh-huángdì.ogg">huángdì</a></span></span></i>) would continue to be borne by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_sovereign" title="Chinese sovereign">Chinese rulers</a> for the next two millennia. Historically, he was often portrayed as a tyrannical ruler and strict <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalist</a>, in part from the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>'s scathing assessments of him. Since the mid 20th-century, scholars have begun to question this evaluation, inciting considerable discussion on the actual nature of his policies and reforms. Regardless, according to sinologist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Loewe" title="Michael Loewe">Michael Loewe</a> "few would contest the view that the achievements of his reign have exercised a paramount influence on the whole of China's subsequent history, marking the start of an epoch that <a href="/wiki/1911_Revolution" title="1911 Revolution">closed in 1911</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe2000654_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe2000654-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Born in the <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">Zhao state</a> capital Handan, as <b>Ying Zheng</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">嬴政</span></span>) or <b>Zhao Zheng</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">趙政</span></span>), his parents were <a href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhuangxiang of Qin">King Zhuangxiang of Qin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Dowager_Zhao" title="Queen Dowager Zhao">Lady Zhao</a>. The wealthy merchant <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Buwei" title="Lü Buwei">Lü Buwei</a> assisted him in succeeding his father as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Qin" title="List of kings of Qin">the ruler of Qin</a>, after which he became <b>Zheng, King of Qin</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">秦王政</span></span>). By the age of 38 in 221 BC, he had conquered all the other <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qin%27s_wars_of_unification" title="Qin's wars of unification">unified all of China</a>, and he ascended the throne as China's first emperor. 
</p><p>During his reign, his generals greatly expanded the size of the Chinese state: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin%27s_campaign_against_the_Yue_tribes" title="Qin's campaign against the Yue tribes">campaigns</a> south of <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a> permanently added the <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Yue</a> lands of <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> to the <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">Chinese cultural orbit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qin%27s_campaign_against_the_Xiongnu" title="Qin's campaign against the Xiongnu">campaigns</a> in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asia</a> conquered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordos_Loop" title="Ordos Loop">Ordos Loop</a> from the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>, although the Xiongnu later rallied under <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a>.
</p><p>Qin Shi Huang also worked with his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_chancellor" title="Chinese chancellor">minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a> to enact major economic and political reforms aimed at the standardization of the diverse practices of the earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_state" title="Ancient Chinese state">Chinese states</a>. He is traditionally said to have <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">banned and burned many books and executed scholars</a>. His public works projects included the incorporation of diverse state walls into a single <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a> and a massive new national road system, as well as his city-sized <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_the_First_Qin_Emperor" title="Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor">mausoleum</a> guarded by a life-sized <a href="/wiki/Terracotta_Army" title="Terracotta Army">Terracotta Army</a>. He ruled until his death in 210 BC, during his fourth tour of Eastern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A7%8B%E7%9A%87%E5%B8%9D" title="wikt:始皇帝">始皇帝</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;">Literal meaning</th><td class="infobox-data">"First <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_emperor" title="Chinese emperor">Emperor</a>"</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Shǐ Huángdì</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Shih<sup>3</sup> Huang<sup>2</sup>-ti<sup>4</sup></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Reconstructions_of_Old_Chinese#Baxter–Sagart_(2014)" title="Reconstructions of Old Chinese">Baxter–Sagart (2014)</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Old Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="och-Latn" style="font-style: normal">* <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">l̥əʔ ɢʷˤaŋ tˤek-s</span></span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Modern Chinese sources often give the personal name of Qin Shi Huang as Ying Zheng, with <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%ADng" title="Yíng">Ying</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">嬴</span></span>) taken as the surname and Zheng (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">政</span></span>) the given name. However, in ancient China, the naming convention differed, and the clan name <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(surname)" title="Zhao (surname)">Zhao</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">趙</span></span>), the place where he was born and raised, may be used as the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">surname</a>. Unlike modern <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese names</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">nobles</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a> had two distinct surnames: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_name" title="Ancestral name">ancestral name</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">姓</span></span>) comprised a larger group descended from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestral veneration in China">prominent ancestor</a>, usually said to have lived during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns_and_Five_Emperors" title="Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors">Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_legend" title="Chinese legend">Chinese legend</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_clan_name" title="Chinese clan name">clan name</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">氏</span></span>) comprised a smaller group that showed a branch's current <a href="/wiki/Fengjian" title="Fengjian">fief</a> or recent title. The ancient practice was to list men's names separately—<a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>'s "Basic Annals of the First Emperor of Qin" introduces him as "given the name Zheng and the surname <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(surname)" title="Zhao (surname)">Zhao</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994127_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994127-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[c]</a></sup>—or to combine the clan surname with the personal name: Sima's account of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Chu" title="State of Chu">Chu</a> describes the sixteenth year of the reign of <a href="/wiki/King_Kaolie_of_Chu" title="King Kaolie of Chu">King Kaolie</a> as "the time when Zhao Zheng was enthroned as King of Qin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994439_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994439-14">[11]</a></sup> However, since modern Chinese surnames (despite usually descending from clan names) use the same character as the old <i>ancestral</i> names, it is much more common in modern Chinese sources to see the emperor's personal name written as Ying Zheng,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[d]</a></sup> using the ancestral name of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Ying" title="House of Ying">the Ying family</a>.
</p><p>The rulers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Qin" title="State of Qin">Qin</a> had styled themselves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_king" title="Chinese king">kings</a> from the time of <a href="/wiki/King_Huiwen_of_Qin" title="King Huiwen of Qin">King Huiwen</a> in 325 BC. Upon his ascension, Zheng became known as the King of Qin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994127_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994127-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qinshibenben_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qinshibenben-12">[10]</a></sup> or King Zheng of Qin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994123_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994123-16">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qinbenben_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qinbenben-17">[13]</a></sup> This title made him the nominal equal of the rulers of <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a>, the <a href="/wiki/King_Nan_of_Zhou" title="King Nan of Zhou">last of whose kings</a> had been deposed by <a href="/wiki/King_Zhaoxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhaoxiang of Qin">King Zhaoxiang of Qin</a> in 256 BC.
</p><p>Following the surrender of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Qi" title="State of Qi">Qi</a> in 221 BC, King Zheng had reunited all of the lands of the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Zhou" title="Kingdom of Zhou">Kingdom of Zhou</a>. Rather than maintain his rank as king, however,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilky_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilky-18">[14]</a></sup> he created a new title of <i>huángdì</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_emperor" title="Chinese emperor">emperor</a>) for himself. This new title combined two titles—<i>huáng</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">mythical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns" title="Three Sovereigns">Three Sovereigns</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">三皇</span></span></span>, <i>Sān Huáng</i>) and the <i>dì</i> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_legend" title="Chinese legend">legendary</a> <a href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns_and_Five_Emperors" title="Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors">Five Emperors</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">五帝</span></span></span>, <i>Wŭ Dì</i>) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_prehistory" title="Chinese prehistory">Chinese prehistory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup> The title was intended to appropriate some of the prestige of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup> whose cult was popular in the later <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> and who was considered to be a founder of the Chinese people. King Zheng chose the new <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a> of First Emperor (<i>Shǐ Huángdì</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wade-Giles" title="Wade-Giles">formerly</a> <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Chinese" title="Romanization of Chinese">transcribed</a> as Shih Huang-ti)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhso5_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhso5-21">[17]</a></sup> on the understanding that his successors would be successively titled the "Second Emperor", "Third Emperor", and so on through the generations. (In fact, the scheme lasted only as long as his immediate heir, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Emperor_of_Qin" title="Second Emperor of Qin">Second Emperor</a>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> The new title carried religious overtones. For that reason, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinologist" title="Sinologist">Sinologists</a>—starting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Boodberg" title="Peter Boodberg">Peter Boodberg</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Edward_H._Schafer" title="Edward H. Schafer">Edward Schafer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup>—sometimes translate it as "thearch" and the First Emperor as the First Thearch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The First Emperor intended that his realm would remain intact through the ages but, following its overthrow and replacement by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> after his death, it became customary to prefix his title with Qin. Thus:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">秦</span></span>, <i>Qín</i> or Ch‘in, "of Qin"</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">始</span></span>, <i>Shǐ</i> or Shih, "first"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">皇帝</span></span>, <i>Huángdì</i> or Huang-ti, "emperor", a new term<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[e]</a></sup> coined from
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">皇</span></span>, <i>Huáng</i> or Huang, literally "shining" or "splendid" and formerly most usually applied "as an epithet of Heaven",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup> a title of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns" title="Three Sovereigns">Three Sovereigns</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">high god</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-herrleeholler_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-herrleeholler-27">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">帝</span></span>, <i>Dì</i> or Ti, the <a href="/wiki/Shangdi" title="Shangdi">high god</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a>, possibly composed of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestral veneration in China">divine ancestors</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> and used by the Zhou as a title of the legendary <a href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns_and_Five_Emperors" title="Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors">Five Emperors</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>As early as <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>, it was common to shorten the resulting four-character Qin Shi Huangdi to <span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">秦始皇</span></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhso6_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhso6-31">[26]</a></sup> variously <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Chinese" title="Romanization of Chinese">transcribed</a> as Qin Shihuang or Qin Shi Huang.
</p><p>Following his elevation as emperor, both Zheng's personal name <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">政</span></span> and possibly its homophone <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">正</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[f]</a></sup> became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Name_taboo" title="Name taboo">taboo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[g]</a></sup> The First Emperor also arrogated the first-person <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_pronoun" title="Chinese pronoun">Chinese pronoun</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">朕</span></span> (<small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>lrəm<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">’</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-35">[28]</a></sup> <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin">mod</a>.</small> <i>zhèn</i>) for his exclusive use and in 212 BC began calling himself The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_immortal" title="Taoist immortal">Immortal</a> <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">真人</span></span>,</span> <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a></small> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>Tin-niŋ</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxsag_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-35">[28]</a></sup> <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin">mod</a>.</small> <i>Zhēnrén</i>, <small>lit.</small> "True Man").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilky_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilky-18">[14]</a></sup> Others were to address him as "Your Majesty" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">陛下</span></span>,</span> <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin">mod</a>.</small> <i>Bìxià</i>, <small>lit.</small> "Beneath the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_palace" title="Chinese palace">Palace</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[29]</a></sup> Steps") in person and "Your Highness" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">上</span></span>) in writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilky_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-wilky-18">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_parentage">Birth and parentage</span></h2>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> during the Han dynasty, the first emperor was the eldest son of the Qin prince Yiren, who later became <a href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhuangxiang of Qin">King Zhuangxiang of Qin</a>. Prince Yiren at that time was residing at the court of <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">Zhao</a>, serving as a hostage to guarantee the <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">armistice</a> between the Qin and Zhao states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59-37">[30]</a></sup> Prince Yiren had fallen in love at first sight with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">concubine</a> of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Buwei" title="Lü Buwei">Lü Buwei</a>, a rich merchant from the <a href="/wiki/Wey_(state)" title="Wey (state)">State of Wey</a>. Lü consented for her to be Yiren's wife, who then became known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Zhao" title="Lady Zhao">Lady Zhao</a> (Zhao Ji) after the state of Zhao. He was given the name Zhao Zheng, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_personal_name" title="Chinese personal name">name</a> Zheng (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%AD%A3" title="wikt:正">正</a></span></span>) came from his month of birth <i>Zhengyue</i>, the first month of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_lunar_calendar" title="Chinese lunar calendar">Chinese lunar calendar</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59-37">[30]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">clan name</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(surname)" title="Zhao (surname)">Zhao</a> came from his father's lineage and was unrelated to either his mother's name or the location of his birth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%AE%8B%E5%BF%A0" title="zh:宋忠">Song Zhong</a> says that his birthday, significantly, was on the first day of Zhengyue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[31]</a></sup>) Lü Buwei's machinations later helped Yiren become <a href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhuangxiang of Qin">King Zhuangxiang of Qin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ren_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ren-39">[32]</a></sup> in 250 BC. 
</p><p>However, the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> also claimed that the first emperor was not the actual son of Prince Yiren but that of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Buwei" title="Lü Buwei">Lü Buwei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huang_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huang-40">[33]</a></sup> According to this account, when Lü Buwei introduced the dancing girl to the prince, she was Lü Buwei's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">concubine</a> and had already become pregnant by him, and the baby was born after an unusually long period of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huang_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huang-40">[33]</a></sup> According to translations of the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCshi_Chunqiu" title="Lüshi Chunqiu">Annals of Lü Buwei</a>, Zhao Ji gave birth to the future emperor in the city of <a href="/wiki/Handan" title="Handan">Handan</a> in 259 BC, the first month of the 48th year of <a href="/wiki/King_Zhaoxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhaoxiang of Qin">King Zhaoxiang of Qin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LuAnnal_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-LuAnnal-41">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that the emperor was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegitimacy" title="Illegitimacy">illegitimate child</a>, widely believed throughout Chinese history, contributed to the generally negative view of the First Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup> However, a number of modern scholars have doubted this account of his birth. Sinologist <a href="/wiki/Derk_Bodde" title="Derk Bodde">Derk Bodde</a> wrote: "There is good reason for believing that the sentence describing this unusual pregnancy is an interpolation added to the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Shih-chi</a></i> by an unknown person in order to slander the First Emperor and indicate his political as well as natal illegitimacy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodde198642–43,_95_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodde198642–43,_95-42">[35]</a></sup> John Knoblock and Jeffrey Riegel, in their translation of Lü Buwei's <i><a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCshi_Chunqiu" title="Lüshi Chunqiu">Spring and Autumn Annals</a></i>, call the story "patently false, meant both to libel Lü and to cast aspersions on the First Emperor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[36]</a></sup> Claiming Lü Buwei—a merchant—as the First Emperor's biological father was meant to be especially disparaging, since later Confucian society regarded merchants as the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">lowest of all social classes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodde198643_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodde198643-44">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_as_the_King_of_Qin">Reign as the King of Qin</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regency">Regency</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="358" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg/150px-Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg/225px-Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg/300px-Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_shihuangdi_c01s06i06.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait painting of Qin Shi Huangdi, first emperor of the Qin Dynasty, from an 18th-century album of Chinese emperors' portraits.</div></div></div>
<p>In 246 BC, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang" title="King Zhuangxiang">King Zhuangxiang</a> died after a short reign of just three years, he was succeeded on the throne by his 13-year-old son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancient_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ancient-45">[38]</a></sup> At the time, Zhao Zheng was still young, so <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Buwei" title="Lü Buwei">Lü Buwei</a> acted as the regent prime minister of the State of Qin, which was still waging war against the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Warring_States" title="Seven Warring States">other six states</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup> Nine years later, in 235 BC, Zhao Zheng assumed full power after Lü Buwei was banished for his involvement in a scandal with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Dowager_Zhao" title="Queen Dowager Zhao">Queen Dowager Zhao</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhao_Chengjiao" title="Zhao Chengjiao">Zhao Chengjiao</a>, the Lord Chang'an (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">长安君</span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-司馬遷《史記·卷043·趙世家》：（赵悼襄王）六年，封长安君以饶。_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-司馬遷《史記·卷043·趙世家》：（赵悼襄王）六年，封长安君以饶。-48">[41]</a></sup> was Zhao Zheng's legitimate half-brother, by the same father but from a different mother. After Zhao Zheng inherited the throne, Chengjiao rebelled at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunliu" title="Tunliu">Tunliu</a> and surrendered to the state of Zhao. Chengjiao's remaining retainers and families were executed by Zhao Zheng.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Lao_Ai.27s_attempted_coup"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lao_Ai's_attempted_coup">Lao Ai's attempted coup</span></h3>
<p>As King Zheng grew older, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Buwei" title="Lü Buwei">Lü Buwei</a> became fearful that the boy king would discover his liaison with his mother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Zhao" title="Lady Zhao">Lady Zhao</a>. He decided to distance himself and look for a replacement for the queen dowager. He found a man named <a href="/wiki/Lao_Ai" title="Lao Ai">Lao Ai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> According to <i>The Record of Grand Historian</i>, Lao Ai was disguised as a <a href="/wiki/Eunuch" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a> by plucking his beard. Later Lao Ai and queen Zhao Ji got along so well they secretly had two sons together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> Lao Ai then became ennobled as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquis" title="Marquis">Marquis</a> Lào Ǎi, and was showered with riches. Lao Ai's plot was supposed to replace King Zheng with one of the hidden sons. But during a dinner party drunken Lào Ǎi was heard bragging about being the young king's step father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> In 238 BC the king was travelling to the former capital, Yong (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">雍</span></span>). Lao Ai seized the queen mother's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_seal" title="Chinese seal">seal</a> and mobilized an army in an attempt to start a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup</a> and rebel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> When King Zheng discovered this fact, he ordered Lü Buwei to let <a href="/wiki/Lord_Changping" title="Lord Changping">Lord Changping</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lord_Changwen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lord Changwen (page does not exist)">Lord Changwen</a> attack Lao Ai. Although the royal army killed hundreds of rebels at the capital, Lao Ai succeeded in fleeing from this battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>A price of 1 million <a href="/wiki/Cash_(Chinese_coin)" title="Cash (Chinese coin)">copper coins</a> was placed on Lao Ai's head if he was taken alive or half a million if dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> Lao Ai's supporters were captured and <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">beheaded</a>; then Lao Ai was tied up and torn to five pieces by horse carriages, while his entire family was executed to the third degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> The two hidden sons were also killed, while mother Zhao Ji was placed under house arrest until her death many years later. Lü Buwei drank a cup of poison wine and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Committed_suicide" title="Committed suicide">committed suicide</a> in 235 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> Ying Zheng then assumed full power as the King of the Qin state. Replacing Lü Buwei, <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a> became the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_China" title="Chancellor of China">chancellor</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_assassination_attempt">First assassination attempt</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jing_Ke" title="Jing Ke">Jing Ke</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="2242" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg/300px-Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg/450px-Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg/600px-Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assassination_attempt_on_Qin_Shi_Huang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jing_Ke" title="Jing Ke">Jing Ke</a>'s assassination attempt on Qin Shi Huang; Jing Ke (left) is held by one of Qin Shi Huang's physicians (left, background). The dagger used in the assassination attempt is seen stuck in the pillar. Qin Shi Huang (right) is seen holding an imperial jade disc. One of his soldier (far right) rushes to save his emperor. Stone rubbing; 3rd century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a></div></div></div>
<p>King Zheng and his troops continued to take over various states. The <a href="/wiki/Yan_(state)" title="Yan (state)">state of Yan</a> was small, weak and frequently harassed by soldiers. It was no match for the Qin state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_Prince_Dan_of_Yan" title="Crown Prince Dan of Yan">Crown Prince Dan of Yan</a> plotted an assassination attempt agains King Zheng, recruiting <a href="/wiki/Jing_Ke" title="Jing Ke">Jing Ke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qin_Wuyang" title="Qin Wuyang">Qin Wuyang</a> for the mission in 227 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ren_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ren-39">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The assassins were supposed to present gifts to King Zheng: a map of Dukang and the severed head of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fan_Wuji" title="Fan Wuji">Fan Wuji</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> Qin Wuyang stepped forward first to present the map case, but was overcome by fear of the king. Jing Ke then advanced with both gifts, while explaining that his partner was trembling because "has never set eyes on the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Son of Heaven</a>". On seeing the dagger unrolled from the map, the king leapt to his feet and struggled to draw his sword, since none of his courtiers was allowed to carry arms in his presence. Jing Ke stabbed at the king but missed, and King Zheng slashed Jing Ke's thigh. In desperation, Jing Ke threw the dagger but missed again, and surrendered after suffering eight wounds. The Yan state was conquered by Qin five years later.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_assassination_attempt">Second assassination attempt</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gao_Jianli" title="Gao Jianli">Gao Jianli</a></div>
<p>Gao Jianli was a close friend of <a href="/wiki/Jing_Ke" title="Jing Ke">Jing Ke</a>, and wanted to avenge his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup> As a famous <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a> player, he was summoned to play for King Zheng. Someone in the palace recognized him and guessed his plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-53">[46]</a></sup> Reluctant to kill such a skilled musician, the emperor ordered his eyes put out, then proceeded with the performance. The king praised Gao Jianli's playing and even allowed him closer. The lute had been weighted with a slab of lead, and Gao Jianli swung it at the king but missed. The second assassination attempt had failed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unification_of_China">Unification of China</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qin%27s_wars_of_unification" title="Qin's wars of unification">Qin's wars of unification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Unification.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qin_Unification.png/450px-Qin_Unification.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qin_Unification.png/675px-Qin_Unification.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qin_Unification.png/900px-Qin_Unification.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Unification.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Qin's unification of seven warring states</div></div></div>
<p>In 230 BC, King Zheng unleashed the final campaigns of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, setting out to conquer the remaining independent kingdoms one by one.
</p><p>The first to fall was <a href="/wiki/Han_(state)" title="Han (state)">Hán</a> (韓; sometimes called Hann to distinguish it from the Hàn 漢 of <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>), in 230 BC. Then Qin took advantage of natural disasters in 229 BC to invade and conquer Zhào, where King Zheng had been born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haw_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haw-55">[48]</a></sup> He now took deadly revenge on those in Zhào who had mistreated him as a child hostage there.
</p><p>Qin armies conquered <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">Zhao</a> in 228 BC, the northern country of <a href="/wiki/Yan_(state)" title="Yan (state)">Yan</a> in 226 BC, the small state of <a href="/wiki/Wei_(state)" title="Wei (state)">Wei</a> in 225 BC, and then <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a>, the largest state and greatest challenge, in 223 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian2007xxxix_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian2007xxxix-56">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 222 BC, the last remnants of Yan and the royal family were captured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liaodong" title="Liaodong">Liaodong</a> in the northeast. The only independent kingdom was <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi</a> in the far east, in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> peninsula. The young king of Qi desperately sent 200,000 men to defend his western borders, but in 221 BC, the Qin armies invaded from the north, captured the king, and annexed Qi. 
</p><p>By 221 BC, all Chinese lands were unified under one powerful ruler, and in the course of conquest, Qin had standardized trade, communication, currency and language. In that same year, King Zheng proclaimed himself with the new title "First Emperor" (始皇帝, <i>Shǐ Huángdì</i>), to symbolize how far he had supassed the achievements of the old Zhou Dynasty rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements200682,_102–03,_131,_134_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements200682,_102–03,_131,_134-57">[50]</a></sup> The Emperor ordered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heshibi" title="Heshibi">Heshibi</a> to be made into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Seal_of_China" title="Imperial Seal of China">Imperial Seal</a>, the Heirloom Seal of the Realm. It was inscribed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sun_Shou&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sun Shou (page does not exist)">Sun Shou</a> with the device of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a>: "Having received the Mandate from Heaven, may he lead a long and prosperous life" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">受命於天, 既壽永昌</span></span>). The Seal became a sigil passed from emperor to emperor.
</p><p>In the South, military expansion continued in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin%27s_campaign_against_the_Yue_tribes" title="Qin's campaign against the Yue tribes">campaigns against the Yue tribes</a>, with various regions  annexed to what is now <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> province, as well as some that are today part of <a href="/wiki/Nanyue" title="Nanyue">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haw_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haw-55">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_as_Emperor_of_Qin">Reign as Emperor of Qin</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_reforms">Administrative reforms</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_administrative_divisions_of_China_before_1912" title="History of the administrative divisions of China before 1912">History of the administrative divisions of China before 1912</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Dynasty.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1010" data-file-width="796" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Qin_Dynasty.png/220px-Qin_Dynasty.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Qin_Dynasty.png/330px-Qin_Dynasty.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Qin_Dynasty.png/440px-Qin_Dynasty.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Dynasty.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Qin dynasty and its administrative divisions</div></div></div>
<p>In an attempt to avoid a recurrence of the political chaos of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, Qin Shi Huang and his prime minister <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a> worked to completely abolish the <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal system</a> of loose alliances and federations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veeck_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Veeck-58">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haw_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Haw-55">[48]</a></sup> They organized the empire into administrative units and subunits: first 36 (later 40) <a href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a> (郡, <i>Jùn</i>), then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counties_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China#History" title="Counties of the People's Republic of China">counties</a> (縣, <i>Xiàn</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Townships_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Townships of the People's Republic of China">townships</a> (鄉, <i>Xiāng</i>) and hundred-family units (里, <i>Li</i>, roughly corresponding to modern-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subdistricts_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Subdistricts of the People's Republic of China">subdistricts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_residential_communities" title="Chinese residential communities">communities</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup> People assigned to these units would no longer be identified by their native region or former feudal state, for example "Chu person" (楚人, <i>Chu rén</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[h]</a></sup> Appointments were to be based on merit instead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hereditary" title="Hereditary">hereditary</a> rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_reforms">Economic reforms</span></h3>
<p>Qin Shi Huang and <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a> unified China economically by standardizing the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_units_of_measurement" title="Chinese units of measurement">Chinese weights and measures</a>. Wagon axles were prescibed a standard length to facilitate road transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veeck_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Veeck-58">[51]</a></sup> The emperor also developed an extensive network of roads and canals for trade and communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veeck_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Veeck-58">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currencies</a> of the different states were standardized to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banliang" title="Banliang">Ban liang coin</a> (半兩, <i>Bàn Liǎng</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup> Perhaps most importantly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese script</a> was unified. Under Li Si, the <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a> of the state of Qin became the official standard, and the Qin script itself was simplified through removal of variant forms. This did away with all the regional scripts to form a universal written language for all of China, despite the diversity of spoken dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism (Chinese philosophy)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">Burning of books and burying of scholars</a></div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title">Later figures</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Sui" title="Emperor Wen of Sui">Emperor Wen of Sui</a></li>
