<li><a href="/wiki/Wang_Anshi" title="Wang Anshi">Wang Anshi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Li_Shanchang" title="Li Shanchang">Li Shanchang</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zhang_Juzheng" title="Zhang Juzheng">Zhang Juzheng</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">Xu Guangqi</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Chinese_Legalism" title="Template:Chinese Legalism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Chinese_Legalism" title="Template talk:Chinese Legalism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Chinese_Legalism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>While the previous Warring States era was one of constant warfare, it was also considered the golden age of free speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-61">[53]</a></sup> Qin Shi Huang eliminated the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a>, which included <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> and other philosophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-61">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[54]</a></sup> With all other philosophies banned, <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">legalism</a> became the mandatory ideology of the Qin dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_59-5"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-59">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning 213 BC, at the instigation of Li Si and to avoid scholars' comparisons of his reign with the past, Qin Shi Huang ordered most existing <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">books to be burned</a>, with the exception of those on astrology, agriculture, medicine, divination, and the history of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">State of Qin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lih_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lih-63">[55]</a></sup> This would also serve to further the ongoing reformation of the writing system by removing examples of obsolete scripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2006131_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2006131-64">[56]</a></sup> Owning the <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Book of Songs</a></i> or the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classic_of_History" title="Classic of History">Classic of History</a></i> was to be punished especially severely. According to the later <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>, the following year Qin Shi Huang had some 460 scholars buried alive for possessing the forbidden books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lih_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lih-63">[55]</a></sup> The emperor's oldest son <a href="/wiki/Fusu" title="Fusu">Fusu</a> criticised him for this act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent research suggests that the "burying of the Confucian scholars alive" is a Confucian martyrs' legend. In fact, the emperor ordered the execution (坑 <i>kēng</i>) of a group of alchemists who had deceived him. In the subsequent Han dynasty, the Confucian scholars, who had served the Qin loyally, used this incident to distance themselves from the failed regime. <a href="/wiki/Kong_Anguo" title="Kong Anguo">Kong Anguo</a> (孔安國 c. 165 – c. 74 BC), a descendant of Confucius, described the alchemists (方士 <i>fāngshì</i>) as Confucianists (儒 <i>rú</i>) and entwined the martyrs' legend with his story of discovering the lost Confucian books behind a demolished wall in his ancestral house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[58]</a></sup> The emperor's own library retained copies of the forbidden books, but most of these were destroyed when <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Yu" title="Xiang Yu">Xiang Yu</a> burned the palaces of <a href="/wiki/Xianyang" title="Xianyang">Xianyang</a> in 206 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200774–75,_119,_148–49_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200774–75,_119,_148–49-67">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Qin Shi Huang also followed the theory of the <a href="/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)">five elements</a>: <a href="/wiki/Fire_(wuxing)" title="Fire (wuxing)">fire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(wuxing)" title="Water (wuxing)">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_(wuxing)" title="Earth (wuxing)">earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wood_(wuxing)" title="Wood (wuxing)">wood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metal_(wuxing)" title="Metal (wuxing)">metal</a>. (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">五德終始說</span></span>) It was believed that the royal house of the previous dynasty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou</a> had ruled by the power of fire, associated with the colour red. The new Qin dynasty must be ruled by the next element on the list, which is water, Zhao Zheng's birth element. Water was represented by the colour black, and black became the preferred colour for Qin garments, flags, and pennants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup> Other associations include north as the <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">cardinal direction</a>, the winter season and the number six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[60]</a></sup> Tallies and official hats were 15 centimetres (5.9 inches) long, carriages two metres (6.6 feet) wide, one <a href="/wiki/Chinese_units_of_measurement" title="Chinese units of measurement">pace</a> (<span lang="zh">步</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Bù</span></i>) was 1.4 metres (4.6 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_assassination_attempt">Third assassination attempt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_(46747095194).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg/220px-Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg/330px-Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg/440px-Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_%2846747095194%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_State_Warring_States_Bronze_Jian_Swords_(46747095194).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze swords (jian), <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Liang_(Western_Han)" title="Zhang Liang (Western Han)">Zhang Liang (Western Han)</a></div>
<p>In 230 BC, the state of Qin had defeated the state of <a href="/wiki/Han_(state)" title="Han (state)">Han</a>. In 218 BC, a former Han aristocrat named <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Liang_(Western_Han)" title="Zhang Liang (Western Han)">Zhang Liang</a> swore revenge on Qin Shih Huang. He sold his valuables and <a href="/wiki/Contract_killing" title="Contract killing">hired</a> a <a href="/wiki/Strongman" title="Strongman">strongman</a> assassin, building a heavy metal cone weighing 120 <a href="/wiki/Catty" title="Catty">jin</a> (roughly 160 lb or 97 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup> The two men hid among the bushes along the emperor's route over a mountain. At a signal, the muscular assassin hurled the cone at the first carriage and shattered it. However, the emperor was travelling with two identical carriages to baffle attackers, and he was actually in the second carriage. Thus the attempt failed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wintle_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wintle-69">[61]</a></sup> though both men were able to escape the subsequent manhunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mah_50-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mah-50">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_works">Public works</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Wall">Great Wall</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a></div>
<p>Numerous state walls had been built during the previous four centuries, many of them closing gaps between river defences and impassable cliffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2006102–103_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2006102–103-70">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup> To impose centralized rule and prevent the resurgence of feudal lords, the Emperor ordered the destruction of walls between the former states, which were now internal walls dividing the empire. 
</p><p>However, to defend against the northern <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> tribes, who had beaten back repeated campaigns against them, he ordered new walls to connect the fortifications along the empire's northern frontier. Hundreds of thousands of workers were mobilized, and an unknown number died, to build this precursor to the current <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[66]</a></sup> Transporting building materials was difficult, so builders always tried to use local materials: rock over mountain ranges, <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> over the plains. "Build and move on" was a guiding principle, implying that the Wall was not a permanently fixed border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[67]</a></sup> There are no surviving records specifying the length and course of the Qin walls, which have largely eroded away over the centuries.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lingqu_Canal">Lingqu Canal</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lingqu" title="Lingqu">Lingqu</a></div>
<p>A South China proverb says: "In the North there is the Great wall, in the South there is the Lingqu canal" (<span lang="zh-Hant">北有長城、南有靈渠</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Běiyǒu chángchéng, nányǒu língqú</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup> In 214 BC the Emperor began the project of a major canal allowing water transport between north and south China, originally for military supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayhew_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayhew-77">[69]</a></sup> The canal, 34 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">kilometres</a> in length, links two of China's major waterways, the <a href="/wiki/Xiang_River" title="Xiang River">Xiang River</a> flowing into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lijiang_River" title="Lijiang River">Li Jiang</a> flowing into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(China)" title="Pearl River (China)">Pearl River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayhew_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mayhew-77">[69]</a></sup> The canal aided Qin's expansion to the south-west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayhew_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mayhew-77">[69]</a></sup> It is considered one of the three great feats of ancient Chinese engineering, along with the Great Wall and the <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dujiangyan_Irrigation_System" title="Dujiangyan Irrigation System">Dujiangyan Irrigation System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayhew_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mayhew-77">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elixir_of_life">Elixir of life</span></h3>
<p>As he grew old, Qin Shi Huang desperately sought the fabled <a href="/wiki/Elixir_of_life" title="Elixir of life">elixir of life</a> which supposedly confers immortality. In his obsessive quest, he fell prey to many fraudulent elixirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ong_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ong-78">[70]</a></sup> He visited <a href="/wiki/Zhifu_Island" title="Zhifu Island">Zhifu Island</a> three times in his search.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one case he sent <a href="/wiki/Xu_Fu" title="Xu Fu">Xu Fu</a>, a Zhifu islander, with ships carrying hundreds of young men and women in search of the mystical <a href="/wiki/Mount_Penglai" title="Mount Penglai">Penglai mountain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wintle_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wintle-69">[61]</a></sup> They sought <a href="/wiki/Anqi_Sheng" title="Anqi Sheng">Anqi Sheng</a>, a thousand-year-old magician who had supposedly invited Qin Shi Huang during a chance meeting during his travels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup> The expedition never returned, perhaps for fear of the consquences of failure. Legends claim that they reached <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and colonized it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ong_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ong-78">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also possible that the Emperor's book burning, which exempted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemical" title="Alchemical">alchemical</a> works, could be seen as an attempt to focus the minds of the best scholars on the Emperor's quest. Some of those buried alive were alchemists, and this could have been a means of testing their death-defying abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2006131,_134_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2006131,_134-81">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emperor built a system of tunnels and passageways to each of his over 200 palaces, because traveling unseen would supposedly keep him safe from evil spirits.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_tours.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Qin_tours.jpg/290px-Qin_tours.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Qin_tours.jpg/435px-Qin_tours.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Qin_tours.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_tours.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Imperial tours of Qin Shi Huang</div></div></div>
<p>In 211 BC a large <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid#Meteors" title="Meteoroid">meteor</a> is said to have fallen in <a href="/wiki/Dong_Commandery" title="Dong Commandery">Dongjun</a> in the lower reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>, and someone inscribed the seditious words "The First Emperor will die and his land will be divided" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">始皇死而地分</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LiangY_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-LiangY-82">[74]</a></sup> The Emperor sent an imperial secretary to investigate this <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a>. No one would confess to the deed, so all living nearby were put to death, and the stone was pulverized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian199335_&amp;_59-37">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his fifth tour of Eastern China, the Emperor became seriously ill in Pingyuanjin (<a href="/wiki/Pingyuan_County,_Shandong" title="Pingyuan County, Shandong">Pingyuan County, Shandong</a>), and died on July–August 210 BC at the palace in Shaqiu <a href="/wiki/Prefecture" title="Prefecture">prefecture</a> (沙丘平台, <i>Shāqiū Píngtái</i>), about two months travel from the capital <a href="/wiki/Xianyang" title="Xianyang">Xianyang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200782._&quot;In_the_seventh_month_on_''bingyin''_the_First_Emperor_passed_away_at_Pingtai_in_Shaqiu.&quot;_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200782._&quot;In_the_seventh_month_on_''bingyin''_the_First_Emperor_passed_away_at_Pingtai_in_Shaqiu.&quot;-83">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cause of Qin Shi Huang's death is unknown, though he was worn down by his many years of rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> It was later alleged that he was <a href="/wiki/Chinese_alchemical_elixir_poisoning" title="Chinese alchemical elixir poisoning">poisoned</a> by an elixir containing <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, given him by his court alchemists and physicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-wright-86">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession">Succession</span></h3>
<p>Upon witnessing the Emperor's death, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a> feared the news could trigger a general uprising, which could take root during the two months travel for the imperial entourage to return to the capital Xianyang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> Li Si decided to hide the death: the only members of the entourage to be informed were a younger son, <a href="/wiki/Qin_Er_Shi" title="Qin Er Shi">Ying Huhai</a>, the eunuch <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Gao" title="Zhao Gao">Zhao Gao</a>, and five or six favourite eunuchs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> Li Si ordered carts of rotten fish to be carried before and behind the wagon of the Emperor, to cover the foul smell from his body decomposing in the summer heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> Pretending he was alive behind the wagon's shade, they changed his clothes daily, brought food, and pretended to carry message to and from him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>After they reached Xianyang, the death of the Emperor was announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup> Qin Shi Huang had not liked to talk about his death and had never written a <a href="/wiki/Will_and_testament" title="Will and testament">wil</a>l.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tung_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tung-87">[79]</a></sup> Although his eldest son <a href="/wiki/Fusu" title="Fusu">Fusu</a> would normally succeed as  emperor, Li Si and the chief eunuch <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Gao" title="Zhao Gao">Zhao Gao</a> conspired to kill Fusu, who was in league with their enemy general <a href="/wiki/Meng_Tian" title="Meng Tian">Meng Tian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tung_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tung-87">[79]</a></sup> Meng Tian's brother, a senior minister, had once punished Zhao Gao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200754_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200754-88">[80]</a></sup> Li Si and Zhao Gao forged a letter from Qin Shi Huang commanding Fusu and General Meng to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tung_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tung-87">[79]</a></sup> The plan worked, and the younger son Hu Hai started his brief reign as the Second Emperor, later known as <a href="/wiki/Qin_Er_Shi" title="Qin Er Shi">Qin Er Shi</a> or "Second Generation Qin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian200715–20,_82,_99-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_emperors_family_tree_(early)#Qin_Dynasty" title="Chinese emperors family tree (early)">Qin dynasty family tree</a></div>
<p>The following are some family members of Qin Shi Huang:
</p>
<ul><li>Parents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2006172_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2006172-89">[81]</a></sup>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhuangxiang of Qin">King Zhuangxiang of Qin</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queen_Dowager_Zhao" title="Queen Dowager Zhao">Queen Dowager Zhao</a></li></ul></li>
<li>Half siblings:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chengjiao_(prince)" title="Chengjiao (prince)">Chengjiao</a>, legitimate paternal half brother from a different mother<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shijichapter6_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-shijichapter6-90">[82]</a></sup> Lord of Chang'an<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-司馬遷《史記·卷043·趙世家》：（赵悼襄王）六年，封长安君以饶。_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-司馬遷《史記·卷043·趙世家》：（赵悼襄王）六年，封长安君以饶。-48">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two illegitimate maternal half-brothers born to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Dowager_Zhao" title="Queen Dowager Zhao">Queen Dowager Zhao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lao_Ai" title="Lao Ai">Lao Ai</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>Children:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fusu" title="Fusu">Fusu</a>, Crown Prince (1st son)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-91">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gao</li>
<li>Jianglü</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qin_Er_Shi" title="Qin Er Shi">Huhai</a>, later <a href="/wiki/Qin_Er_Shi" title="Qin Er Shi">Qin Er Shi</a> (18th son)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-91">[83]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Qin Shi Huang had about 50 children (about 30 sons and 15 daughters), but most of their names are unknown. He had numerous <a href="/wiki/Concubinage" title="Concubinage">concubines</a> but appeared to have never named an empress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mausoleum">Mausoleum</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_the_First_Qin_Emperor" title="Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor">Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Terracotta_Army" title="Terracotta Army">Terracotta Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qin_bronze_chariot" title="Qin bronze chariot">Qin bronze chariot</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Army_of_Terracotta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Army_of_Terracotta.jpg/290px-Army_of_Terracotta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Army_of_Terracotta.jpg/435px-Army_of_Terracotta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Army_of_Terracotta.jpg/580px-Army_of_Terracotta.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Army_of_Terracotta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lifelike <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> soldier statues from the <a href="/wiki/Terracotta_Army" title="Terracotta Army">Terracotta Army</a>, discovered near modern <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, which was meant to guard the <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_the_First_Qin_Emperor" title="Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor">Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese historian <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>, writing a century after the First Emperor's death, wrote that it took 700,000 men to construct the <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_the_First_Qin_Emperor" title="Mausoleum of the First Qin Emperor">emperor's mausoleum</a>. British historian <a href="/wiki/John_Man_(author)" title="John Man (author)">John Man</a> points out that this figure is larger than the population of any city in the world at that time and he calculates that the foundations could have been built by 16,000 men in two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup> While Sima Qian never mentioned the <a href="/wiki/Terracotta_Army" title="Terracotta Army">terracotta army</a>, the statues were discovered by a group of farmers digging wells on 29 March 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup> The soldiers were created with a series of mix-and-match clay molds and then further individualized by the artists' hand. <a href="/wiki/Han_purple_and_Han_blue" title="Han purple and Han blue">Han Purple</a> was also used on some of the warriors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup>
There are around 6,000 Terracotta Warriors and their purpose was to protect the Emperor in the afterlife from evil spirits. Also among the army are chariots and 40,000 real bronze weapons.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_96-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-96'>[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the first projects which the young king accomplished while he was alive was the construction of his own tomb. In 215 BC Qin Shi Huang ordered General <a href="/wiki/Meng_Tian" title="Meng Tian">Meng Tian</a> to begin its construction with the assistance of 300,000 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-wood-26">[22]</a></sup> Other sources suggest that he ordered 720,000 unpaid laborers to build his tomb according to his specifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancient_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ancient-45">[38]</a></sup> Again, given John Man's observation regarding populations at the time (see paragraph above), these historical estimates are debatable. The main tomb (located at <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Qin_Shi_Huang&amp;params=34_22_53_N_109_15_13_E_type:landmark"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">34°22′53″N</span> <span class="longitude">109°15′13″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">34.38139°N 109.25361°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">34.38139; 109.25361</span></span></span></a></span>) containing the emperor has yet to be opened and there is evidence suggesting that it remains relatively intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal2007207_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal2007207-97">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>'s description of the tomb includes replicas of palaces and scenic towers, "rare utensils and wonderful objects", 100 rivers made with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, representations of "the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">heavenly bodies</a>", and <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbows</a> rigged to shoot anyone who tried to break in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> The tomb was built at the foot of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Li" title="Mount Li">Mount Li</a>, 30 kilometers away from <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>. Modern archaeologists have located the tomb, and have inserted probes deep into it. The probes revealed abnormally high quantities of mercury, some 100 times the naturally occurring rate, suggesting that some parts of the legend are credible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-wright-86">[78]</a></sup> Secrets were maintained, as most of the workmen who built the tomb were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-wright-86">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation_and_assessment">Reputation and assessment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qinshihuang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2220" data-file-width="1732" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Qinshihuang.jpg/220px-Qinshihuang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Qinshihuang.jpg/330px-Qinshihuang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Qinshihuang.jpg/440px-Qinshihuang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qinshihuang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A posthumous depiction of Qin Shi Huang, painted during the late <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>Traditional Chinese <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a> almost always portrayed the First Emperor of the Chinese unified states as a brutal tyrant who had an obsessive fear of assassination. Ideological antipathy towards the <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalist</a> State of Qin was established as early as 266 BC, when Confucian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Xun_Kuang" title="Xun Kuang">Xunzi</a> disparaged it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Later Confucian historians condemned the emperor, alleging that he <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">burned the classics and buried Confucian scholars alive</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> They eventually compiled a list of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ten_Crimes_of_Qin" title="Ten Crimes of Qin">Ten Crimes of Qin</a></i> to highlight his tyrannical actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The famous Han poet and statesman <a href="/wiki/Jia_Yi" title="Jia Yi">Jia Yi</a> concluded his essay <i>The Faults of Qin</i> (過秦論, <i>Guò Qín Lùn</i>) with what was to become the standard Confucian judgment of the reasons for Qin's collapse. Jia Yi's essay, admired as a masterpiece of rhetoric and reasoning, was copied into two great Han histories and has had a far-reaching influence on Chinese political thought as a classic illustration of Confucian theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup> He attributed Qin's disintegration to its internal failures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup> Jia Yi wrote that:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Qin, from a tiny base, had become a great power, ruling the land and receiving homage from all quarters for a hundred odd years. Yet after they unified the land and secured themselves within the pass, a single common rustic could nevertheless challenge this empire... Why? Because the ruler lacked humaneness and rightness; because preserving power differs fundamentally from seizing power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In more modern times, historical assessment of the First Emperor different from traditional Chinese historiography began to emerge. The reassessment was spurred on by the <a href="/wiki/Century_of_humiliation" title="Century of humiliation">weakness of China in the latter half of the 19th century and early 20th century</a>. At that time some began to regard Confucian traditions as an impediment to China's entry into the modern world, opening the way for changing perspectives.
</p><p>At a time when foreign nations encroached upon Chinese territory, leading <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Xiao_Yishan" title="Xiao Yishan">Xiao Yishan</a> emphasized the role of Qin Shi Huang in repulsing the northern barbarians, particularly in the construction of the Great Wall.
</p><p>Another historian, Ma Feibai (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">馬非百</span></span>), published in 1941 a full-length <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist</a> biography of the First Emperor entitled <i>Qín Shǐ Huángdì Zhuàn</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">秦始皇帝傳</span></span>), calling him "one of the great heroes of Chinese history". Ma compared him with the contemporary leader <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> and saw many parallels in the careers and policies of the two men, both of whom he admired. Chiang's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Expedition_(1926%E2%80%931927)" title="Northern Expedition (1926–1927)">Northern Expedition</a> of the late 1920s, which directly preceded the new Nationalist government at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> was compared to the unification brought about by Qin Shi Huang.
</p><p>With the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Communist Revolution</a> and the establishment of a new, revolutionary regime in 1949, another re-evaluation of the First Emperor emerged as a Marxist critique. This new interpretation of Qin Shi Huang was generally a combination of traditional and modern views, but essentially critical. This is exemplified in the <i>Complete History of China</i>, which was compiled in September 1955 as an official survey of Chinese history. The work described the First Emperor's major steps toward unification and standardisation as corresponding to the interests of the ruling group and the <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">merchant</a> <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">class</a>, not of the nation or the people, and the subsequent fall of his dynasty as a manifestation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle">class struggle</a>. The perennial debate about the fall of the Qin Dynasty was also explained in Marxist terms, the <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasant</a> rebellions being a revolt against oppression—a revolt which undermined the dynasty, but which was bound to fail because of a compromise with "<a href="/wiki/Landlord" title="Landlord">landlord</a> class elements".
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2781" data-file-width="1945" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg/220px-Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg/330px-Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg/440px-Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qin_Shi_Huang_statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Emperor Qin Shi Huang in <a href="/wiki/Handan" title="Handan">Handan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since 1972, however, a radically different official view of Qin Shi Huang in accordance with <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoist</a> thought has been given prominence throughout China. Hong Shidi's biography <i>Qin Shi Huang</i> initiated the re-evaluation. The work was published by the state press as a mass popular history, and it sold 1.85 million copies within two years. In the new era, Qin Shi Huang was seen as a far-sighted ruler who destroyed the forces of division and established the first unified, centralized state in Chinese history by rejecting the past. Personal attributes, such as his quest for immortality, so emphasized in traditional historiography, were scarcely mentioned. The new evaluations described approvingly how, in his time (an era of great political and social change), he had no compunctions against using violent methods to crush <a href="/wiki/Counter-revolutionary" title="Counter-revolutionary">counter-revolutionaries</a>, such as the "industrial and commercial slave owner" chancellor Lü Buwei. However, he was criticized for not being as thorough as he should have been, and as a result, after his death, hidden subversives under the leadership of the chief eunuch <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Gao" title="Zhao Gao">Zhao Gao</a> were able to seize power and use it to restore the old feudal order.
</p><p>To round out this re-evaluation, Luo Siding put forward a new interpretation of the precipitous collapse of the Qin Dynasty in an article entitled "On the Class Struggle During the Period Between Qin and Han" in a 1974 issue of <i>Red Flag</i>, to replace the old explanation. The new theory claimed that the cause of the fall of Qin lay in the lack of thoroughness of Qin Shi Huang's "<a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorship</a> over the reactionaries, even to the extent of permitting them to worm their way into organs of political authority and usurp important posts."
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> was reviled for his persecution of intellectuals. On being compared to the First Emperor, Mao boasted:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He buried 460 scholars alive; we have buried forty-six thousand scholars alive... You [intellectuals] revile us for being Qin Shi Huangs. You are wrong. We have surpassed Qin Shi Huang a hundredfold. When you berate us for imitating his despotism, we are happy to agree! Your mistake was that you did not say so enough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Tom Ambrose characterises Qin Shi Huang as the founder of "the first police state in history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"The Wall and the Books" ("<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">La muralla y los libros</i></span>"), an acclaimed essay on Qin Shi Huang published by <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a> (1899–1986) in the 1952 collection <i>Other Inquisitions</i> (<i><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Otras Inquisiciones</i></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_Shadow" title="The Emperor's Shadow">The Emperor's Shadow</a></i> (1996) – The film focuses on Qin Shi Huang's relationship with the musician <a href="/wiki/Gao_Jianli" title="Gao Jianli">Gao Jianli</a>, a friend of the assassin <a href="/wiki/Jing_Ke" title="Jing Ke">Jing Ke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Emperor_and_the_Assassin" title="The Emperor and the Assassin">The Emperor and the Assassin</a></i> (1999) – The film covers much of Ying Zheng's career, recalling his early experiences as a hostage and foreshadowing his dominance over China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-110">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hero_(2002_film)" title="Hero (2002 film)">Hero</a></i> (2002) – The film stars <a href="/wiki/Jet_Li" title="Jet Li">Jet Li</a>, a nameless assassin who plans an assassination attempt on the King of Qin (<a href="/wiki/Chen_Daoming" title="Chen Daoming">Chen Daoming</a>). The film is a fictional re-imagining of the assassination attempt by Jing Ke on Qin Shi Huang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Great_Wall" title="Rise of the Great Wall">Rise of the Great Wall</a></i> (1986) – a 63-episode Hong Kong TV series chronicling the events from the emperor's birth until his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Liu" title="Tony Liu">Tony Liu</a> played Qin Shi Huang.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/A_Step_into_the_Past" title="A Step into the Past">A Step into the Past</a></i> (2001) – a Hong Kong <a href="/wiki/TVB" title="TVB">TVB</a> production based on a <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> novel by <a href="/wiki/Huang_Yi_(author)" title="Huang Yi (author)">Huang Yi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang_(2001_TV_series)" title="Qin Shi Huang (2001 TV series)">Qin Shi Huang</a></i> (2002) – a mainland Chinese TV semi-fictionalized series with <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Fengyi" title="Zhang Fengyi">Zhang Fengyi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(manga)" title="Kingdom (manga)">Kingdom</a></i> (2006) – a Japanese manga that provides a fictionalized account of the unification of China by Ying Zheng with <a href="/wiki/Li_Xin_(Qin)" title="Li Xin (Qin)">Li Xin</a> and all the people that contributed to the conquest of the six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Fate/Grand_Order" title="Fate/Grand Order">Fate/Grand Order</a></i> (2015), an online, free-to-play role-playing mobile game of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate/Stay_Night" title="Fate/Stay Night">Fate</a> franchise developed by Delightworks and published by <a href="/wiki/Aniplex" title="Aniplex">Aniplex</a> features Qin Shi Huang as a Ruler class servant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>First Emperor: The Man Who Made China</i> (2006) – a drama-documentary special about Qin Shi Huang. <a href="/wiki/James_Pax" title="James Pax">James Pax</a> played the emperor. It was shown on <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=China%27s_First_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="China's First Emperor (page does not exist)">China's First Emperor</a></i> (2008) – a special three-hour documentary by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">The History Channel</a>. Xu Pengkai played Qin Shi Huang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Mummy:_Tomb_of_the_Dragon_Emperor" title="The Mummy: Tomb of the Dragon Emperor">The Mummy: Tomb of the Dragon Emperor</a></i> (2008) - the third of <a href="/wiki/The_Mummy_(franchise)" title="The Mummy (franchise)"><i>The Mummy</i> trilogy</a>. It happened that after General Ming Guo was killed for touching Zi Yuan, she put a curse on the Emperor and his army.</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This 19th-century posthumous depiction is from a Korean book now kept in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2006Between_pp._76–77_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2006Between_pp._76–77-1">[1]</a></sup> It is based on a portrait of Qin Shi Huang from the <i><a href="/wiki/Sancai_Tuhui" title="Sancai Tuhui">Sancai Tuhui</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200729_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200729-2">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-end-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-end_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though <a href="#CITEREFVervoorn1990">Vervoorn 1990</a>, p. 311 gives 18 July 210 as the date for the end of Qin Shi Huang's reign, this is not corroborated in other sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe2000823_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe2000823-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbieri-LowYates2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1W3sCgAAQBAJpgPR19_xix]_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbieri-LowYates2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1W3sCgAAQBAJpgPR19_xix]-5">[4]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In simplified Chinese, <span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">及生，名为政，姓赵氏</span></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qinshibenben_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qinshibenben-12">[10]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, e.g., Nienhauser's gloss of the name Zhao Zheng (n. 579).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994439_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESima_Qian1994439-14">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While the specific title was new, also note the use of <span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">皇天上帝</span></span></span> ("August <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Heaven</a> <a href="/wiki/Shangdi" title="Shangdi">Shangdi</a>"), a conflation of the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> gods by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Zhou" title="Duke of Zhou">Duke of Zhou</a> used in his addresses to the conquered Shang peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-herrleeholler_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-herrleeholler-27">[23]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That both were forbidden has been the general understanding of historians but Beck cites numerous sources from the era employing the latter character in support of the argument that it was not forbidden until the reign of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Emperor_of_Qin" title="Second Emperor of Qin"><i>Second</i> Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loserbaby_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-loserbaby-32">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/King_Zhuangxiang_of_Qin" title="King Zhuangxiang of Qin">His father</a>'s name <span class="nowrap"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">子楚</span></span></span> also became taboo, prompting references to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Chu" title="State of Chu">Chu</a> to be replaced by its original name "Jing" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">荆</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loserbaby_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-loserbaby-32">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The source also mentions ch'ien-shou was the new name of the Qin people. The may be the Wade-Giles romanization of (秦受, <i>Qín shòu</i>) "subjects of the Qin empire".</span>
</li>
